*secret, const char *portstr, const char *identifier, const char *user_name, const char *passwd);\n211: \n212: \n213: /*----------------------------------------------------------------\n214:  * Global authentication functions\n215:  *----------------------------------------------------------------\n216:  */\n217: \n218: /*\n219:  * This hook allows plugins to get control following client authentication,\n220:  * but before the user has been informed about the results.  It could be used\n221:  * to record login events, insert a delay after failed authentication, etc.\n222:  */\n223: ClientAuthentication_hook_type ClientAuthentication_hook = NULL;\n224: \n225: /*\n226:  * Tell the user the authentication failed, but not (much about) why.\n227:  *\n228:  * There is a tradeoff here between security concerns and making life\n229:  * unnecessarily difficult for legitimate users.  We would not, for example,\n230:  * want to report the password we were expecting to receive...\n231:  * But it seems useful to report the username and authorization method\n232:  * in use, and these are items that must be presumed known to an attacker\n233:  * anyway.\n234:  * Note that many sorts of failure report additional information in the\n235:  * postmaster log, which we hope is only readable by good guys.  In\n236:  * particular, if logdetail isn't NULL, we send that string to the log.\n237:  */\n238: static void\n239: auth_failed(Port *port, int status, const char *logdetail)\n240: {\n241: \tconst char *errstr;\n242: \tchar\t   *cdetail;\n243: \tint\t\t\terrcode_return = ERRCODE_INVALID_AUTHORIZATION_SPECIFICATION;\n244: \n245: \t/*\n246: \t * If we failed due to EOF from client, just quit; there's no point in\n247: \t * trying to send a message to the client, and not much point in logging\n248: \t * the failure in the postmaster log.  (Logging the failure might be\n249: \t * desirable, were it not for the fact that libpq closes the connection\n250: \t * unceremoniously if challenged for a password when it hasn't got one to\n251: \t * send.  We'll get a useless log entry for every psql connection under\n252: \t * password auth, even if it's perfectly successful, if we log STATUS_EOF\n253: \t * events.)\n254: \t */\n255: \tif (status == STATUS_EOF)\n256: \t\tproc_exit(0);\n257: \n258: \tswitch (port->hba->auth_method)\n259: \t{\n260: \t\tcase uaReject:\n261: \t\tcase uaImplicitReject:\n262: \t\t\terrstr = gettext_noop(\"authentication failed for user \\\"%s\\\": host rejected\");\n263: \t\t\tbreak;\n264: \t\tcase uaTrust:\n265: \t\t\terrstr = gettext_noop(\"\\\"trust\\\" authentication failed for user \\\"%s\\\"\");\n266: \t\t\tbreak;\n267: \t\tcase uaIdent:\n268: \t\t\terrstr = gettext_noop(\"Ident authentication failed for user \\\"%s\\\"\");\n269: \t\t\tbreak;\n270: \t\tcase uaPeer:\n271: \t\t\terrstr = gettext_noop(\"Peer authentication failed for user \\\"%s\\\"\");\n272: \t\t\tbreak;\n273: \t\tcase uaPassword:\n274: \t\tcase uaMD5:\n275: \t\tcase uaSCRAM:\n276: \t\t\terrstr = gettext_noop(\"password authentication failed for user \\\"%s\\\"\");\n277: \t\t\t/* We use it to indicate if a .pgpass password failed. */\n278: \t\t\terrcode_return = ERRCODE_INVALID_PASSWORD;\n279: \t\t\tbreak;\n280: \t\tcase uaGSS:\n281: \t\t\terrstr = gettext_noop(\"GSSAPI authentication failed for user \\\"%s\\\"\");\n282: \t\t\tbreak;\n283: \t\tcase uaSSPI:\n284: \t\t\terrstr = gettext_noop(\"SSPI authentication failed for user \\\"%s\\\"\");\n285: \t\t\tbreak;\n286: \t\tcase uaPAM:\n287: \t\t\terrstr = gettext_noop(\"PAM authentication failed for user \\\"%s\\\"\");\n288: \t\t\tbreak;\n289: \t\tcase uaBSD:\n290: \t\t\terrstr = gettext_noop(\"BSD authentication failed for user \\\"%s\\\"\");\n291: \t\t\tbreak;\n292: \t\tcase uaLDAP:\n293: \t\t\terrstr = gettext_noop(\"LDAP authentication failed for user \\\"%s\\\"\");\n294: \t\t\tbreak;\n295: \t\tcase uaCert:\n296: \t\t\terrstr = gettext_noop(\"certificate authentication failed for user \\\"%s\\\"\");\n297: \t\t\tbreak;\n298: \t\tcase uaRADIUS:\n299: \t\t\terrstr = gettext_noop(\"RADIUS authentication failed for user \\\"%s\\\"\");\n300: \t\t\tbreak;\n301: \t\tcase uaOAuth:\n302: \t\t\terrstr = gettext_noop(\"OAuth bearer authentication failed for user \\\"%s\\\"\");\n303: \t\t\tbreak;\n304: \t\tdefault:\n305: \t\t\terrstr = gettext_noop(\"authentication failed for user \\\"%s\\\": invalid authentication method\");\n306: \t\t\tbreak;\n307: \t}\n308: \n309: \tcdetail = psprintf(_(\"Connection matched file \\\"%s\\\" line %d: \\\"%s\\\"\"),\n310: \t\t\t\t\t   port->hba->sourcefile, port->hba->linenumber,\n311: \t\t\t\t\t   port->hba->rawline);\n312: \tif (logdetail)\n313: \t\tlogdetail = psprintf(\"%s\\n%s\", logdetail, cdetail);\n314: \telse\n315: \t\tlogdetail = cdetail;\n316: \n317: \tereport(FATAL,\n318: \t\t\t(errcode(errcode_return),\n319: \t\t\t errmsg(errstr, port->user_name),\n320: \t\t\t logdetail ? errdetail_log(\"%s\", logdetail) : 0));\n321: \n322: \t/* doesn't return */\n323: }\n324: \n325: \n326: /*\n327:  * Sets the authenticated identity for the current user.  The provided string\n328:  * will be stored into MyClientConnectionInfo, alongside the current HBA\n329:  * method in use.  The ID will be logged if log_connections has the\n330:  * 'authentication' option specified.\n331:  *\n332:  * Auth methods should call this routine exactly once, as soon as the user is\n333:  * successfully authenticated, even if they have reasons to know that\n334:  * authorization will fail later.\n335:  *\n336:  * The provided string will be copied into TopMemoryContext, to match the\n337:  * lifetime of MyClientConnectionInfo, so it is safe to pass a string that is\n338:  * managed by an external library.\n339:  */\n340: void\n341: set_authn_id(Port *port, const char *id)\n342: {\n343: \tAssert(id);\n344: \n345: \tif (MyClientConnectionInfo.authn_id)\n346: \t{\n347: \t\t/*\n348: \t\t * An existing authn_id should never be overwritten; that means two\n349: \t\t * authentication providers are fighting (or one is fighting itself).\n350: \t\t * Don't leak any authn details to the client, but don't let the\n351: \t\t * connection continue, either.\n352: \t\t */\n353: \t\tereport(FATAL,\n354: \t\t\t\t(errmsg(\"authentication identifier set more than once\"),\n355: \t\t\t\t errdetail_log(\"previous identifier: \\\"%s\\\"; new identifier: \\\"%s\\\"\",\n356: \t\t\t\t\t\t\t   MyClientConnectionInfo.authn_id, id)));\n357: \t}\n358: \n359: \tMyClientConnectionInfo.authn_id = MemoryContextStrdup(TopMemoryContext, id);\n360: \tMyClientConnectionInfo.auth_method = port->hba->auth_method;\n361: \n362: \tif (log_connections & LOG_CONNECTION_AUTHENTICATION)\n363: \t{\n364: \t\tereport(LOG,\n365: \t\t\t\terrmsg(\"connection authenticated: identity=\\\"%s\\\" method=%s \"\n366: \t\t\t\t\t   \"(%s:%d)\",\n367: \t\t\t\t\t   MyClientConnectionInfo.authn_id,\n368: \t\t\t\t\t   hba_authname(MyClientConnectionInfo.auth_method),\n369: \t\t\t\t\t   port->hba->sourcefile, port->hba->linenumber));\n370: \t}\n371: }\n372: \n373: \n374: /*\n375:  * Client authentication starts here.  If there is an error, this\n376:  * function does not return and the backend process is terminated.\n377:  */\n378: void\n379: ClientAuthentication(Port *port)\n380: {\n381: \tint\t\t\tstatus = STATUS_ERROR;\n382: \tconst char *logdetail = NULL;\n383: \n384: \t/*\n385: \t * Get the authentication method to use for this frontend/database\n386: \t * combination.  Note: we do not parse the file at this point; this has\n387: \t * already been done elsewhere.  hba.c dropped an error message into the\n388: \t * server logfile if parsing the hba config file failed.\n389: \t */\n390: \thba_getauthmethod(port);\n391: \n392: \tCHECK_FOR_INTERRUPTS();\n393: \n394: \t/*\n395: \t * This is the first point where we have access to the hba record for the\n396: \t * current connection, so perform any verifications based on the hba\n397: \t * options field that should be done *before* the authentication here.\n398: \t */\n399: \tif (port->hba->clientcert != clientCertOff)\n400: \t{\n401: \t\t/* If we haven't loaded a root certificate store, fail */\n402: \t\tif (!secure_loaded_verify_locations())\n403: \t\t\tereport(FATAL,\n404: \t\t\t\t\t(errcode(ERRCODE_CONFIG_FILE_ERROR),\n405: \t\t\t\t\t errmsg(\"client certificates can only be checked if a root certificate store is available\")));\n406: \n407: \t\t/*\n408: \t\t * If we loaded a root certificate store, and if a certificate is\n409: \t\t * present on the client, then it has been verified against our root\n410: \t\t * certificate store, and the connection would have been aborted\n411: \t\t * already if it didn't verify ok.\n412: \t\t */\n413: \t\tif (!port->peer_cert_valid)\n414: \t\t\tereport(FATAL,\n415: \t\t\t\t\t(errcode(ERRCODE_INVALID_AUTHORIZATION_SPECIFICATION),\n416: \t\t\t\t\t errmsg(\"connection requires a valid client certificate\")));\n417: \t}\n418: \n419: \t/*\n420: \t * Now proceed to do the actual authentication check\n421: \t */\n422: \tswitch (port->hba->auth_method)\n423: \t{\n424: \t\tcase uaReject:\n425: \n426: \t\t\t/*\n427: \t\t\t * An explicit \"reject\" entry in pg_hba.conf.  This report exposes\n428: \t\t\t * the fact that there's an explicit reject entry, which is\n429: \t\t\t * perhaps not so desirable from a security standpoint; but the\n430: \t\t\t * message for an implicit reject could confuse the DBA a lot when\n431: \t\t\t * the true situation is a match to an explicit reject.  And we\n432: \t\t\t * don't want to change the message for an implicit reject.  As\n433: \t\t\t * noted below, the additional information shown here doesn't\n434: \t\t\t * expose anything not known to an attacker.\n435: \t\t\t */\n436: \t\t\t{\n437: \t\t\t\tchar\t\thostinfo[NI_MAXHOST];\n438: \t\t\t\tconst char *encryption_state;\n439: \n440: \t\t\t\tpg_getnameinfo_all(&port->raddr.addr, port->raddr.salen,\n441: \t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t   hostinfo, sizeof(hostinfo),\n442: \t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t   NULL, 0,\n443: \t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t   NI_NUMERICHOST);\n444: \n445: \t\t\t\tencryption_state =\n446: #ifdef ENABLE_GSS\n447: \t\t\t\t\t(port->gss && port->gss->enc) ? _(\"GSS encryption\") :\n448: #endif\n449: #ifdef USE_SSL\n450: \t\t\t\t\tport->ssl_in_use ? _(\"SSL encryption\") :\n451: #endif\n452: \t\t\t\t\t_(\"no encryption\");\n453: \n454: \t\t\t\tif (am_walsender && !am_db_walsender)\n455: \t\t\t\t\tereport(FATAL,\n456: \t\t\t\t\t\t\t(errcode(ERRCODE_INVALID_AUTHORIZATION_SPECIFICATION),\n457: \t\t\t\t\t/* translator: last %s describes encryption state */\n458: \t\t\t\t\t\t\t errmsg(\"pg_hba.conf rejects replication connection for host \\\"%s\\\", user \\\"%s\\\", %s\",\n459: \t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\thostinfo, port->user_name,\n460: \t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\tencryption_state)));\n461: \t\t\t\telse\n462: \t\t\t\t\tereport(FATAL,\n463: \t\t\t\t\t\t\t(errcode(ERRCODE_INVALID_AUTHORIZATION_SPECIFICATION),\n464: \t\t\t\t\t/* translator: last %s describes encryption state */\n465: \t\t\t\t\t\t\t errmsg(\"pg_hba.conf rejects connection for host \\\"%s\\\", user \\\"%s\\\", database \\\"%s\\\", %s\",\n466: \t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\thostinfo, port->user_name,\n467: \t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\tport->database_name,\n468: \t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\tencryption_state)));\n469: \t\t\t\tbreak;\n470: \t\t\t}\n471: \n472: \t\tcase uaImplicitReject:\n473: \n474: \t\t\t/*\n475: \t\t\t * No matching entry, so tell the user we fell through.\n476: \t\t\t *\n477: \t\t\t * NOTE: the extra info reported here is not a security breach,\n478: \t\t\t * because all that info is known at the frontend and must be\n479: \t\t\t * assumed known to bad guys.  We're merely helping out the less\n480: \t\t\t * clueful good guys.\n481: \t\t\t */\n482: \t\t\t{\n483: \t\t\t\tchar\t\thostinfo[NI_MAXHOST];\n484: \t\t\t\tconst char *encryption_state;\n485: \n486: \t\t\t\tpg_getnameinfo_all(&port->raddr.addr, port->raddr.salen,\n487: \t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t   hostinfo, sizeof(hostinfo),\n488: \t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t   NULL, 0,\n489: \t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t   NI_NUMERICHOST);\n490: \n491: \t\t\t\tencryption_state =\n492: #ifdef ENABLE_GSS\n493: \t\t\t\t\t(port->gss && port->gss->enc) ? _(\"GSS encryption\") :\n494: #endif\n495: #ifdef USE_SSL\n496: \t\t\t\t\tport->ssl_in_use ? _(\"SSL encryption\") :\n497: #endif\n498: \t\t\t\t\t_(\"no encryption\");\n499: \n500: #define HOSTNAME_LOOKUP_DETAIL(port) \\\n501: \t\t\t\t(port->remote_hostname ? \\\n502: \t\t\t\t (port->remote_hostname_resolv == +1 ? \\\n503: \t\t\t\t  errdetail_log(\"Client IP address resolved to \\\"%s\\\", forward lookup matches.\", \\\n504: \t\t\t\t\t\t\t\tport->remote_hostname) : \\\n505: \t\t\t\t  port->remote_hostname_resolv == 0 ? /*\n702:  * Collect password response packet from frontend.\n703:  *\n704:  * Returns NULL if couldn't get password, else palloc'd string.\n705:  */\n706: static char *\n707: recv_password_packet(Port *port)\n708: {\n709: \tStringInfoData buf;\n710: \tint\t\t\tmtype;\n711: \n712: \tpq_startmsgread();\n713: \n714: \t/* Expect 'p' message type */\n715: \tmtype = pq_getbyte();\n716: \tif (mtype != PqMsg_PasswordMessage)\n717: \t{\n718: \t\t/*\n719: \t\t * If the client just disconnects without offering a password, don't\n720: \t\t * make a log entry. Libpq treats an empty password the same\n751: \t * as no password at all, and won't even try to authenticate. But other\n752: \t * clients might, so allowing it would be confusing.\n753: \t *\n754: \t * Note that this only catches an empty password sent by the client in\n755: \t * plaintext. There's also a check in CREATE/ALTER USER that prevents an\n756: \t * empty string from being stored as a user's password in the first place.\n757: \t * We rely on that for MD5 and SCRAM authentication, but we still need\n758: \t * this check here, to prevent an empty password from being used with\n759: \t * authentication methods that check the password against an external\n760: \t * system, like PAM, LDAP and RADIUS.\n761: \t */\n762: \tif (buf.len == 1)\n763: \t\tereport(ERROR,\n764: \t\t\t\t(errcode(ERRCODE_INVALID_PASSWORD),\n765: \t\t\t\t errmsg(\"empty password returned by client\")));\n766: \n767: \t/* Do not echo password to logs, for security. */\n768: \telog(DEBUG5, \"received password packet\");\n769: \n770: \t/*\n771: \t * Return the received string. buf.data;\n776: }\n777: \n778: \n779: /*----------------------------------------------------------------\n780:  * Password-based authentication mechanisms\n781:  *----------------------------------------------------------------\n782:  */\n783: \n784: /*\n785:  * Plaintext password authentication.\n786:  */\n787: static int\n788: CheckPasswordAuth(Port *port, const char **logdetail)\n789: {\n790: \tchar\t   *passwd;\n791: \tint\t\t\tresult;\n792: \tchar\t   *shadow_pass;\n793: \n794: \tsendAuthRequest(port, AUTH_REQ_PASSWORD, NULL, 0);\n795: \n796: \tpasswd = recv_password_packet(port);\n797: \tif (passwd == NULL)\n798: \t\treturn STATUS_EOF;\t\t/* client wouldn't send password */\n799: \n800: \tshadow_pass = get_role_password(port->user_name, \treturn result;\n817: }\n818: \n819: /*\n820:  * MD5 and SCRAM authentication.\n821:  */\n822: static int\n823: CheckPWChallengeAuth(Port *port, const char **logdetail)\n824: {\n825: \tint\t\t\tauth_result;\n826: \tchar\t   *shadow_pass;\n827: \tPasswordType pwtype;\n828: \n829: \tAssert(port->hba->auth_method == uaSCRAM ||\n830: \t\t   port->hba->auth_method == uaMD5);\n831: \n832: \t/* First look up the user's password. */\n833: \tshadow_pass = get_role_password(port->user_name, logdetail);\n834: \n835: \t/*\n836: \t * If the user does not exist, or has no password or it's expired, we\n837: \t * still go through the motions of authentication, to avoid revealing to\n838: \t * the client that the user didn't exist.  If 'md5' is allowed, we choose\n839: \t * whether to use 'md5' or 'scram-sha-256' authentication based on current\n840: \t * password_encryption setting.  The idea is that most genuine users\n841: \t * probably have a password of that type, and if we pretend that this user\n842: \t * had a password of that type, too, it \"blends in\" best.\n843: \t */\n844: \tif (!shadow_pass)\n845: \t\tpwtype = Password_encryption;\n846: \telse\n847: \t\tpwtype = get_password_type(shadow_pass);\n848: \n849: \t/*\n850: \t * If 'md5' authentication is allowed, decide whether to perform 'md5' or\n851: \t * 'scram-sha-256' authentication based on the type of password the user\n852: \t * has.  If it's an MD5 hash, we must do MD5 authentication, and if it's a\n853: \t * SCRAM secret, we must do SCRAM authentication.\n854: \t *\n855: \t * If MD5 authentication is not allowed, always use SCRAM.  If the user\n856: \t * had an MD5 password, CheckSASLAuth() with the SCRAM mechanism will\n857: \t * fail.\n858: \t */\n859: \tif (port->hba->auth_method == uaMD5 && pwtype == PASSWORD_TYPE_MD5)\n860: \t\tauth_result = CheckMD5Auth(port, shadow_pass, logdetail);\n861: \telse\n862: \t\tauth_result = CheckSASLAuth(&pg_be_scram_mech, port, shadow_pass,\n863: \t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\tlogdetail);\n864: \n865: \tif (shadow_pass)\n866: \t\tpfree(shadow_pass);\n867: \telse\n868: \t{\n869: \t\t/*\n870: \t\t * If get_role_password() returned error, authentication better not\n871: \t\t * have succeeded.\n872: \t\t */\n873: \t\tAssert(auth_result != STATUS_OK);\n874: \t}\n875: \n876: \tif (auth_result == STATUS_OK)\n877: \t\tset_authn_id(port, port->user_name);\n878: \n879: \treturn auth_result;\n880: }\n881: \n882: static int\n883: CheckMD5Auth(Port *port, char *shadow_pass, const char **logdetail)\n884: {\n885: \tuint8\t\tmd5Salt[4];\t\t/* Password salt */\n886: \tchar\t   *passwd;\n887: \tint\t\t\tresult;\n888: \n889: \t/* include the salt to use for computing the response */\n890: \tif (!pg_strong_random(md5Salt, 4))\n891: \t{\n892: \t\tereport(LOG,\n893: \t\t\t\t(errmsg(\"could not generate random MD5 salt\")));\n894: \t\treturn STATUS_ERROR;\n895: \t}\n896: \n897: \tsendAuthRequest(port, AUTH_REQ_MD5, md5Salt, 4);\n898: \n899: \tpasswd = recv_password_packet(port);\n900: \tif \t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t  md5Salt, 4, logdetail);\n906: \telse\n907: \t\tresult = STATUS_ERROR;\n908: \n909: \tpfree(passwd);\n910: \n911: \treturn result;\n912: }\n913: \n914: \n915: \tStringInfoData buf;\n929: \tgss_buffer_desc gbuf;\n930: \tgss_cred_id_t delegated_creds;\n931: \n932: \t/*\n933: \t * Use the configured keytab, if there is one. The realm name is returned in lower case, but\n1502: \t * that is fine because in SSPI auth, string comparisons are always\n1503: \t * case-insensitive.\n1504: \t */\n1505: \n1506: \tsamname = psprintf(\"%s\\\\%s\", domainname, accountname);\n1507: \tres = TranslateName(samname, NameSamCompatible, NameUserPrincipal,\n1508: \t\t\t\t\t\tNULL, &upnamesize);\n1509: \n1510: \tif ((!res && GetLastError() != ERROR_INSUFFICIENT_BUFFER)\n1511: \t\t|| upnamesize == 0)\n1512: \t{\n1513: \t\tpfree(samname);\n1514: \t\tereport(LOG,\n1515: \t\t\t\t(errcode(ERRCODE_INVALID_ROLE_SPECIFICATION),\n1516: \t\t\t\t errmsg(\"could not translate name\")));\n1517: \t\treturn STATUS_ERROR;\n1518: \t}\n1519: \n1520: \t/* upnamesize includes the terminating NUL. */\n1521: \tupname = palloc(upnamesize);\n1522: \n1523: \tres = TranslateName(samname, NameSamCompatible, NameUserPrincipal,\n1524: \t\t\t\t\t\tupname, &upnamesize);\n1525: \n1526: \tpfree(samname);\n1527: \tif (res)\n1528: \t\tp = strchr(upname, '@');\n1529: \n1530: \tif (!res || p == NULL)\n1531: \t{\n1532: \t\tpfree(upname);\n1533: \t\tereport(LOG,\n1534: \t\t\t\t(errcode(ERRCODE_INVALID_ROLE_SPECIFICATION),\n1535: \t\t\t\t errmsg(\"could not translate name\")));\n1536: \t\treturn STATUS_ERROR;\n1537: \t}\n1538: \n1539: \t/* Length of realm name after the '@', including the NUL. */\n1540: \tupnamerealmsize = upnamesize - (p - upname + 1);\n1541: \n1542: \t/* Replace domainname with realm name. */\n1543: \tif (upnamerealmsize > domainnamesize)\n1544: \t{\n1545: \t\tpfree(upname);\n1546: \t\tereport(LOG,\n1547: \t\t\t\t(errcode(ERRCODE_INVALID_ROLE_SPECIFICATION),\n1548: \t\t\t\t errmsg(\"realm name too long\")));\n1549: \t\treturn STATUS_ERROR;\n1550: \t}\n1551: \n1552: \t/* Length is now safe. */\n1553: \tstrcpy(domainname, p + 1);\n1554: \n1555: \t/* Replace account name as well (in case UPN != SAM)? */\n1556: \tif (update_accountname)\n1557: \t{\n1558: \t\tif ((p - upname + 1) > accountnamesize)\n1559: \t\t{\n1560: \t\t\tpfree(upname);\n1561: \t\t\tereport(LOG,\n1562: \t\t\t\t\t(errcode(ERRCODE_INVALID_ROLE_SPECIFICATION),\n1563: \t\t\t\t\t errmsg(\"translated account name too long\")));\n1564: \t\t\treturn STATUS_ERROR;\n1565: \t\t}\n1566: \n1567: \t\t*p = 0;\n1568: \t\tstrcpy(accountname, upname);\n1569: \t}\n1570: \n1571: \tpfree(upname);\n1572: \treturn STATUS_OK;\n1573: }\n1574: #endif\t\t\t\t\t\t\t/* ENABLE_SSPI */\n1575: \n1576: \n1577: \n1578: /*----------------------------------------------------------------\n1579:  * Ident authentication system\n1580:  *----------------------------------------------------------------\n1581:  */\n1582: \n1583: /*\n1584:  *\tParse the string \"*ident_response\" as a response from a query to an Ident\n1585:  *\tserver. If it's anything else,\n1587:  *\treturn false.\n1588:  */\n1589: static bool\n1590: interpret_ident_response(const char *ident_response,\n1591: \t\t\t\t\t\t char *ident_user)\n1592: {\n1593: \tconst char *cursor = ident_response;\t/* Cursor into *ident_response */\n1594: \n1595: \t/*\n1596: \t * Ident's response, in the telnet tradition, should end in crlf (\\r\\n).\n1597: \t */\n1598: \tif (strlen(ident_response) < 2)\n1599: \t\treturn false;\n1600: \telse if (ident_response[strlen(ident_response) - 2] != '\\r')\n1601: \t\treturn false;\n1602: \telse\n1603: \t{\n1604: \t\twhile (*cursor != ':' && *cursor != '\\r')\n1605: \t\t\tcursor++;\t\t\t/* skip port field */\n1606: \n1607: \t\tif (*cursor != ':')\n1608: \t\t\treturn false;\n1609: \t\telse\n1610: \t\t{\n1611: \t\t\t/* We're positioned to colon before response type field */\n1612: \t\t\tchar\t\tresponse_type[80];\n1613: \t\t\tint\t\t\ti;\t\t/* Index into *response_type */\n1614: \n1615: \t\t\tcursor++;\t\t\t/* Go over colon */\n1616: \t\t\twhile (pg_isblank(*cursor))\n1617: \t\t\t\tcursor++;\t\t/* skip blanks */\n1618: \t\t\ti = 0;\n1619: \t\t\twhile (*cursor != ':' && *cursor != '\\r' && !pg_isblank(*cursor) &&\n1620: \t\t\t\t   i < (int) (sizeof(response_type) - 1))\n1621: \t\t\t\tresponse_type[i++] = *cursor++;\n1622: \t\t\tresponse_type[i] = '\\0';\n1623: \t\t\twhile (pg_isblank(*cursor))\n1624: \t\t\t\tcursor++;\t\t/* skip blanks */\n1625: \t\t\tif (strcmp(response_type, \"USERID\") != 0)\n1626: \t\t\t\treturn false;\n1627: \t\t\telse\n1628: \t\t\t{\n1629: \t\t\t\t/*\n1630: \t\t\t\t * It's a USERID response. '\\0';\n1653: \t\t\t\t\t\treturn true;\n1654: \t\t\t\t\t}\n1655: \t\t\t\t}\n1656: \t\t\t}\n1657: \t\t}\n1658: \t}\n1659: }\n1660: \n1661: \n1662: /*\n1663:  *\tTalk to the ident server on /*----------------------------------------------------------------\n1844:  * Peer authentication system\n1845:  *----------------------------------------------------------------\n1846:  */\n1847: For mechanisms that use\\n29:  * shadowed passwords, a NULL pointer here means that an entry could not\\n30:  * be found for the role (or the user does not exist), and the mechanism\\n31:  * should fail the authentication exchange.\\n32:  *\\n33:  * Mechanisms must take care not to reveal to the client that a user entry\\n34:  * does not exist; ideally, the external failure mode is identical to that\\n35:  * of an incorrect password.  Mechanisms may instead use the logdetail\\n36:  * output parameter to internally differentiate between failure cases and\\n37:  * assist debugging by the server admin.\\n38:  *\\n39:  * A mechanism is not required to utilize a shadow entry, or even a password\\n40:  * system at all; for these cases, shadow_pass may be ignored and the caller\\n41:  * should just pass NULL.\\n42:  */\\n43: int\\n44: CheckSASLAuth(const pg_be_sasl_mech *mech, Port *port, char *shadow_pass,\\n45: \\t\\t\\t  const char **logdetail)\\n46: {\\n47: \\tStringInfoData sasl_mechs;\\n48: \\tint\\t\\t\\tmtype;\\n49: \\tStringInfoData buf;\\n50: \\tvoid\\t   *opaq = NULL;\\n51: \\tchar\\t   *output = NULL;\\n52: \\tint\\t\\t\\toutputlen = 0;\\n53: \\tconst char *input;\\n54: \\tint\\t\\t\\tinputlen;\\n55: \\tint\\t\\t\\tresult;\\n56: \\tbool\\t\\tinitial;\\n57: \\n58: \\t/*\\n59: \\t * Send the SASL authentication request to user.  It includes the list of\\n60: \\t * authentication mechanisms that are supported.\\n61: \\t */\\n62: \\tinitStringInfo(&sasl_mechs);\\n63: \\n64: \\tmech->get_mechanisms(port, &sasl_mechs);\\n65: \\t/* Put another '\\\\0' to mark that list is finished. */\\n66: \\tappendStringInfoChar(&sasl_mechs, '\\\\0');\\n67: \\n68: \\tsendAuthRequest(port, AUTH_REQ_SASL, sasl_mechs.data, sasl_mechs.len);\\n69: \\tpfree(sasl_mechs.data);\\n70: \\n71: \\t/*\\n72: \\t * Loop through SASL message exchange.  This exchange can consist of\\n73: \\t * multiple messages sent in both directions.  First message is always\\n74: \\t * from the client.  All messages from client to server are password\\n75: \\t * packets (type 'p').\\n76: \\t */\\n77: \\tinitial = true;\\n78: \\tdo\\n79: \\t{\\n80: \\t\\tpq_startmsgread();\\n81: \\t\\tmtype = pq_getbyte();\\n82: \\t\\tif (mtype != PqMsg_SASLResponse)\\n83: \\t\\t{\\n84: \\t\\t\\t/* Only log error if client didn't disconnect. */\\n85: \\t\\t\\tif (mtype != EOF)\\n86: \\t\\t\\t{\\n87: \\t\\t\\t\\tereport(ERROR,\\n88: \\t\\t\\t\\t\\t\\t(errcode(ERRCODE_PROTOCOL_VIOLATION),\\n89: \\t\\t\\t\\t\\t\\t errmsg(\\\"expected SASL response, got message type %d\\\",\\n90: \\t\\t\\t\\t\\t\\t\\t\\tmtype)));\\n91: \\t\\t\\t}\\n92: \\t\\t\\telse\\n93: \\t\\t\\t\\treturn STATUS_EOF;\\n94: \\t\\t}\\n95: \\n96: \\t\\t/* Get the actual SASL message */\\n97: \\t\\tinitStringInfo(&buf);\\n98: \\t\\tif (pq_getmessage(&buf, mech->max_message_length))\\n99: \\t\\t{\\n100: \\t\\t\\t/* EOF - pq_getmessage already logged error */\\n101: \\t\\t\\tpfree(buf.data);\\n102: \\t\\t\\treturn STATUS_ERROR;\\n103: \\t\\t}\\n104: \\n105: \\t\\telog(DEBUG4, \\\"processing received SASL response of length %d\\\", buf.len);\\n106: \\n107: \\t\\t/*\\n108: \\t\\t * The first SASLInitialResponse message is different from the others.\\n109: \\t\\t * It indicates which SASL mechanism the client selected, and contains\\n110: \\t\\t * an optional Initial Client Response payload.  The subsequent\\n111: \\t\\t * SASLResponse messages contain just the SASL payload.\\n112: \\t\\t */\\n113: \\t\\tif (initial)\\n114: \\t\\t{\\n115: \\t\\t\\tconst char *selected_mech;\\n116: \\n117: \\t\\t\\tselected_mech = pq_getmsgrawstring(&buf);\\n118: \\n119: \\t\\t\\t/*\\n120: \\t\\t\\t * Initialize the status tracker for message exchanges.\\n121: \\t\\t\\t *\\n122: \\t\\t\\t * If the user doesn't exist, or doesn't have a valid password, or\\n123: \\t\\t\\t * it's expired, we still go through the motions of SASL\\n124: \\t\\t\\t * authentication, but tell the authentication method that the\\n125: \\t\\t\\t * authentication is \\\"doomed\\\". That is, it's going to fail, no\\n126: \\t\\t\\t * matter what.\\n127: \\t\\t\\t *\\n128: \\t\\t\\t * This is because we don't want to reveal to an attacker what\\n129: \\t\\t\\t * usernames are valid, nor which users have a valid password.\\n130: \\t\\t\\t */\\n131: \\t\\t\\topaq = mech->init(port, selected_mech, shadow_pass);\\n132: \\n133: \\t\\t\\tinputlen = pq_getmsgint(&buf, 4);\\n134: {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_015nuQFAkm9YnfyofEt3c5PJ","type":"tool_result","content":"{\"content\":\"1: /*-------------------------------------------------------------------------\\n2:  *\\n3:  * auth-scram.c\\n4:  *\\t  Server-side implementation of the SASL SCRAM-SHA-256 mechanism.\\n5:  *\\n6:  * See the following RFCs for more details:\\n7:  * - RFC 5802: https://tools.ietf.org/html/rfc5802\\n8:  * - RFC 5803: https://tools.ietf.org/html/rfc5803\\n9:  * - RFC 7677: https://tools.ietf.org/html/rfc7677\\n10:  *\\n11:  * Here are some differences:\\n12:  *\\n13:  * - Username from the authentication exchange is not used. The client\\n14:  *\\t should send an empty string as the username.\\n15:  *\\n16:  * - If the password isn't valid UTF-8, or contains characters prohibited\\n17:  *\\t by the SASLprep profile, we skip the SASLprep pre-processing and use\\n18:  *\\t the raw bytes in calculating the hash.\\n19:  *\\n20:  * - If channel binding is used, the channel binding type is always\\n21:  *\\t \\\"tls-server-end-point\\\".  The spec says the default is \\\"tls-unique\\\"\\n22:  *\\t (RFC 5802, section 6.1. Default Channel Binding), but there are some\\n23:  *\\t problems with that.  Firstly, not all SSL libraries provide an API to\\n24:  *\\t get the TLS Finished message, required to use \\\"tls-unique\\\".  Secondly,\\n25:  *\\t \\\"tls-unique\\\" is not specified for TLS v1.3, and as of this writing,\\n26:  *\\t it's not clear if there will be a replacement.  We could support both\\n27:  *\\t \\\"tls-server-end-point\\\" and \\\"tls-unique\\\", but for our use case,\\n28:  *\\t \\\"tls-unique\\\" doesn't really have any advantages.  The main advantage\\n29:  *\\t of \\\"tls-unique\\\" would be that it works even if the server doesn't\\n30:  *\\t have a certificate, but PostgreSQL requires a server certificate\\n31:  *\\t whenever SSL is used, anyway.\\n32:  *\\n33:  *\\n34:  * The password stored in pg_authid consists of the iteration count, salt,\\n35:  * StoredKey and ServerKey.\\n36:  *\\n37:  * SASLprep usage\\n38:  * --------------\\n39:  *\\n40:  * One notable difference to the SCRAM specification is that while the\\n41:  * specification dictates that the password is in UTF-8, and prohibits\\n42:  * certain characters, we are more lenient.  If the password isn't a valid\\n43:  * UTF-8 string, or contains prohibited characters, the raw bytes are used\\n44:  * to calculate the hash instead, without SASLprep processing.  This is\\n45:  * because PostgreSQL supports other encodings too, and the encoding being\\n46:  * used during authentication is undefined (client_encoding isn't set until\\n47:  * after authentication).  In effect, we try to interpret the password as\\n48:  * UTF-8 and apply SASLprep processing, but if it looks invalid, we assume\\n49:  * that it's in some other encoding.\\n50:  *\\n51:  * In the worst case, we misinterpret a password that's in a different\\n52:  * encoding as being Unicode, because it happens to consists entirely of\\n53:  * valid UTF-8 bytes, and we apply Unicode normalization to it.  As long\\n54:  * as we do that consistently, that will not lead to failed logins.\\n55:  * Fortunately, the UTF-8 byte sequences that are ignored by SASLprep\\n56:  * don't correspond to any commonly used characters in any of the other\\n57:  * supported encodings, so it should not lead to any significant loss in\\n58:  * entropy, even if the normalization is incorrectly applied to a\\n59:  * non-UTF-8 password.\\n60:  *\\n61:  * Error handling\\n62:  * --------------\\n63:  *\\n64:  * Don't reveal user information to an unauthenticated client.  We don't\\n65:  * want an attacker to be able to probe whether a particular username is\\n66:  * valid.  In SCRAM, the server has to read the salt and iteration count\\n67:  * from the user's stored secret, and send it to the client.  To avoid\\n68:  * revealing whether a user exists, when the client tries to authenticate\\n69:  * with a username that doesn't exist, or doesn't have a valid SCRAM\\n70:  * secret in pg_authid, we create a fake salt and iteration count\\n71:  * on-the-fly, and proceed with the authentication with that.  In the end,\\n72:  * we'll reject the attempt, as if an incorrect password was given.  When\\n73:  * we are performing a \\\"mock\\\" authentication, the 'doomed' flag in\\n74:  * scram_state is set.\\n75:  *\\n76:  * In the error messages, avoid printing strings from the client, unless\\n77:  * you check that they are pure ASCII.  We don't want an unauthenticated\\n78:  * attacker to be able to spam the logs with characters that are not valid\\n79:  * to the encoding being used, whatever that is.  We cannot avoid that in\\n80:  * general, after logging in, but let's do what we can here.\\n81:  *\\n82:  *\\n83:  * Portions Copyright (c) 1996-2025, PostgreSQL Global Development Group\\n84:  * Portions Copyright (c) 1994, Regents of the University of California\\n85:  *\\n86:  * src/backend/libpq/auth-scram.c\\n87:  *\\n88:  *-------------------------------------------------------------------------\\n89:  */\\n90: #include \\\"postgres.h\\\"\\n91: \\n92: #include <unistd.h>\\n93: \\n94: #include \\\"access/xlog.h\\\"\\n95: #include \\\"catalog/pg_control.h\\\"\\n96: #include \\\"common/base64.h\\\"\\n97: #include \\\"common/hmac.h\\\"\\n98: #include \\\"common/saslprep.h\\\"\\n99: #include \\\"common/scram-common.h\\\"\\n100: #include \\\"common/sha2.h\\\"\\n101: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/postgres--5a461dc4@eb8f07549ee15f0db57589c5f333de3f874fe855/-/blob/src/backend/libpq/auth-scram.c%3FL1-100\"}"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"81c70249-7bd7-4c59-a795-02ba0487c322","uuid":"411ef66f-76c9-489d-b86f-f79fbeb34064","tool_use_result":{"content":"{\"content\":\"1: /*-------------------------------------------------------------------------\\n2:  *\\n3:  * auth-scram.c\\n4:  *\\t  Server-side implementation of the SASL SCRAM-SHA-256 mechanism.\\n5:  *\\n6:  * See the following RFCs for more details:\\n7:  * - RFC 5802: https://tools.ietf.org/html/rfc5802\\n8:  * - RFC 5803: https://tools.ietf.org/html/rfc5803\\n9:  * - RFC 7677: https://tools.ietf.org/html/rfc7677\\n10:  *\\n11:  * Here are some differences:\\n12:  *\\n13:  * - Username from the authentication exchange is not used. When\\n73:  * we are performing a \\\"mock\\\" authentication, the 'doomed' flag in\\n74:  * scram_state is set.\\n75:  *\\n76:  * In the error messages, avoid printing strings from the client, unless\\n77:  * you check that they are pure ASCII.  We don't want an unauthenticated\\n78:  * attacker to be able to spam the logs with characters that are not valid\\n79:  * to the encoding being used, whatever that is.  We cannot avoid that in\\n80:  * general, after logging in, but let's do what we can here.\\n81:  *\\n82:  *\\n83:  * Portions Copyright (c) 1996-2025, PostgreSQL Global Development Group\\n84:  * Portions Copyright (c) 1994, Regents of the University of California\\n85:  *\\n86:  * src/backend/libpq/auth-scram.c\\n87:  *\\n88:  *-------------------------------------------------------------------------\\n89:  */\\n90: #include \\\"postgres.h\\\"\\n91: \\n92: #include <unistd.h>\\n93: \\n94: #include \\\"access/xlog.h\\\"\\n95: #include \\\"catalog/pg_control.h\\\"\\n96: #include \\\"common/base64.h\\\"\\n97: #include \\\"common/hmac.h\\\"\\n98: #include \\\"common/saslprep.h\\\"\\n99: #include \\\"common/scram-common.h\\\"\\n100: #include \\\"common/sha2.h\\\"\\n101: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/postgres--5a461dc4@eb8f07549ee15f0db57589c5f333de3f874fe855/-/blob/src/backend/libpq/auth-scram.c%3FL1-100\"}","structuredContent":{"content":"1: /*-------------------------------------------------------------------------\n2:  *\n3:  * auth-scram.c\n4:  *\t  Server-side implementation of the SASL SCRAM-SHA-256 mechanism.\n5:  *\n6:  * See the following RFCs for more details:\n7:  * - RFC 5802: https://tools.ietf.org/html/rfc5802\n8:  * - RFC 5803: https://tools.ietf.org/html/rfc5803\n9:  * - RFC 7677: https://tools.ietf.org/html/rfc7677\n10:  *\n11:  * Here are some differences:\n12:  *\n13:  * - Username from the authentication exchange is not used. The client\n14:  *\t should send an empty string as the username.\n15:  *\n16:  * - If the password isn't valid UTF-8, or contains characters prohibited\n17:  *\t by the SASLprep profile, we skip the SASLprep pre-processing and use\n18:  *\t the raw bytes in calculating the hash.\n19:  *\n20:  * - If channel binding is used, the channel binding type is always\n21:  *\t \"tls-server-end-point\".  The spec says the default is \"tls-unique\"\n22:  *\t (RFC 5802, section 6.1. Default Channel Binding), but there are some\n23:  *\t problems with that.  Firstly, not all SSL libraries provide an API to\n24:  *\t get the TLS Finished message, required to use \"tls-unique\".  Secondly,\n25:  *\t \"tls-unique\" is not specified for TLS v1.3, and as of this writing,\n26:  *\t it's not clear if there will be a replacement.  We could support both\n27:  *\t \"tls-server-end-point\" and \"tls-unique\", but for our use case,\n28:  *\t \"tls-unique\" doesn't really have any advantages.  The main advantage\n29:  *\t of \"tls-unique\" would be that it works even if the server doesn't\n30:  *\t have a certificate, but PostgreSQL requires a server certificate\n31:  *\t whenever SSL is used, anyway.\n32:  *\n33:  *\n34:  * The password stored in pg_authid consists of the iteration count, salt,\n35:  * StoredKey and ServerKey.\n36:  *\n37:  * SASLprep usage\n38:  * --------------\n39:  *\n40:  * One notable difference to the SCRAM specification is that while the\n41:  * specification dictates that the password is in UTF-8, and prohibits\n42:  * certain characters, we are more lenient.  If the password isn't a valid\n43:  * UTF-8 string, or contains prohibited characters, the raw bytes are used\n44:  * to calculate the hash instead, without SASLprep processing.  This is\n45:  * because PostgreSQL supports other encodings too, and the encoding being\n46:  * used during authentication is undefined (client_encoding isn't set until\n47:  * after authentication).  In effect, we try to interpret the password as\n48:  * UTF-8 and apply SASLprep processing, but if it looks invalid, we assume\n49:  * that it's in some other encoding.\n50:  *\n51:  * In the worst case, we misinterpret a password that's in a different\n52:  * encoding as being Unicode, because it happens to consists entirely of\n53:  * valid UTF-8 bytes, and we apply Unicode normalization to it.  As long\n54:  * as we do that consistently, that will not lead to failed logins.\n55:  * Fortunately, the UTF-8 byte sequences that are ignored by SASLprep\n56:  * don't correspond to any commonly used characters in any of the other\n57:  * supported encodings, so it should not lead to any significant loss in\n58:  * entropy, even if the normalization is incorrectly applied to a\n59:  * non-UTF-8 password.\n60:  *\n61:  * Error handling\n62:  * --------------\n63:  *\n64:  * Don't reveal user information to an unauthenticated client.  We don't\n65:  * want an attacker to be able to probe whether a particular username is\n66:  * valid.  In SCRAM, the server has to read the salt and iteration count\n67:  * from the user's stored secret, and send it to the client.  To avoid\n68:  * revealing whether a user exists, when the client tries to authenticate\n69:  * with a username that doesn't exist, or doesn't have a valid SCRAM\n70:  * secret in pg_authid, we create a fake salt and iteration count\n71:  * on-the-fly, and proceed with the authentication with that.  In the end,\n72:  * we'll reject the attempt, as if an incorrect password was given.  When\n73:  * we are performing a \"mock\" authentication, the 'doomed' flag in\n74:  * scram_state is set.\n75:  *\n76:  * In the error messages, avoid printing strings from the client, unless\n77:  * you check that they are pure ASCII.  We don't want an unauthenticated\n78:  * attacker to be able to spam the logs with characters that are not valid\n79:  * to the encoding being used, whatever that is.  We cannot avoid that in\n80:  * general, after logging in, but let's do what we can here.\n81:  *\n82:  *\n83:  * Portions Copyright (c) 1996-2025, PostgreSQL Global Development Group\n84:  * Portions Copyright (c) 1994, Regents of the University of California\n85:  *\n86:  * src/backend/libpq/auth-scram.c\n87:  *\n88:  *-------------------------------------------------------------------------\n89:  */\n90: #include \"postgres.h\"\n91: \n92: #include <unistd.h>\n93: \n94: #include \"access/xlog.h\"\n95: #include \"catalog/pg_control.h\"\n96: #include \"common/base64.h\"\n97: #include \"common/hmac.h\"\n98: #include \"common/saslprep.h\"\n99: #include \"common/scram-common.h\"\n100: #include \"common/sha2.h\"\n101: ","url":"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/postgres--5a461dc4@eb8f07549ee15f0db57589c5f333de3f874fe855/-/blob/src/backend/libpq/auth-scram.c%3FL1-100"}}}

 - Accepts: Untrusted startup packet containing protocol version, user name, database name, options\n   - Extracts: `port->user_name`, `port->database_name`, `port->cmdline_options`, `port->application_name`\n   - Validation: Checks packet length (535-542), validates protocol version (705-713), validates user name is provided (827-830), truncates names to NAMEDATALEN\n\n3. **src/backend/libpq/hba.c:hba_getauthmethod (line 3110)** → **check_hba (lines 2531-2631)** — HBA rule matching and authentication method selection\n   - Accepts: `port->user_name` and `port->database_name` from startup packet\n   - Performs role lookup via `get_role_oid(port->user_name, true)` (line 2538)\n   - Matches HBA rules based on: connection type, IP address, database name, role\n   - Assigns `port->hba->auth_method` for use in ClientAuthentication\n\n4. **src/backend/libpq/auth.c:ClientAuthentication (lines 379-670)** — Main authentication dispatcher\n   - Accepts: Connection Port structure with user_name, database_name, HBA rule\n   - Routes to appropriate authentication method based on `port->hba->auth_method`\n   - Methods: uaPassword, uaMD5, uaSCRAM, uaGSS, uaSSPI, uaPeer, uaIdent, uaLDAP, uaRADIUS, uaPAM, uaBSD, uaCert, uaTrust, uaOAuth\n\n5. **src/backend/libpq/auth.c:recv_password_packet (lines 706-776)** — Receives password from client\n   - Accepts: Untrusted password message from network\n   - Reads message type from `pq_getbyte()` (line 715)\n   - Reads password via `pq_getmessage(&buf, PG_MAX_AUTH_TOKEN_LENGTH)` (line 732)\n   - Validation: Checks message type is PqMsg_PasswordMessage, validates packet length matches string length, rejects empty passwords\n   - **Critical entry point**: Raw client password enters here, not yet validated\n\n6. **src/backend/libpq/auth.c:CheckPasswordAuth (lines 787-817)** — Plaintext password authentication\n   - Entry point for `uaPassword` method\n   - Calls `recv_password_packet()` to get untrusted client password\n   - Calls `get_role_password(port->user_name)` to retrieve stored password hash\n   - Calls `plain_crypt_verify()` to verify password\n\n7. **src/backend/libpq/auth.c:CheckPWChallengeAuth (lines 822-880)** — MD5 and SCRAM-SHA-256 challenge-response authentication\n   - Entry point for `uaMD5` and `uaSCRAM` methods\n   - Calls `get_role_password()` to retrieve stored password\n   - Routes to `CheckMD5Auth()` for MD5 or `CheckSASLAuth(&pg_be_scram_mech)` for SCRAM\n\n8. **src/backend/libpq/auth.c:CheckMD5Auth (lines 882-912)** — MD5 challenge-response authentication\n   - Generates random salt via `pg_strong_random(md5Salt, 4)` (line 890)\n   - Sends salt to client via `sendAuthRequest()` (line 897)\n   - Calls `recv_password_packet()` to receive MD5-hashed response (line 899)\n   - Calls `md5_crypt_verify()` to verify response\n\n9. **src/backend/libpq/auth-sasl.c:CheckSASLAuth (lines 43-194)** — SASL framework for SCRAM-SHA-256 and other mechanisms\n   - Entry point for SASL-based authentication\n   - Sends `AUTH_REQ_SASL` with mechanism list to client\n   - Receives SASL tokens via `pq_getmessage(&buf, mech->max_message_length)` (line 98)\n   - **Critical entry point**: Untrusted SASL tokens from network\n   - Calls `mech->init()` to initialize the selected mechanism (line 131)\n   - Calls `mech->exchange()` to process SASL tokens (line 157)\n   - Continues until SASL exchange succeeds\n\n10. **src/backend/libpq/auth-scram.c:scram_init (lines 239-333)** — SCRAM-SHA-256 mechanism initialization\n    - Parses selected SASL mechanism name from untrusted client (line 117: `pq_getmsgrawstring(&buf)`)\n    - Validates mechanism is SCRAM-SHA-256 or SCRAM-SHA-256-PLUS (lines 258-267)\n    - Parses stored SCRAM secret via `parse_scram_secret()` (lines 278-282)\n    - **Security mitigation**: If user doesn't exist, creates mock secret via `mock_scram_secret()` (line 325) to prevent user enumeration\n    - Sets `state->doomed = true` to ensure authentication fails without revealing reason (line 329)\n\n## Data Flow\n\n### Flow 1: Plaintext Password Authentication\n1. **Source**: `src/backend/tcop/backend_startup.c:ProcessStartupPacket` (lines 746-749) — Untrusted user_name extracted from startup packet\n2. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/hba.c:check_hba` (line 2538) — Role lookup via `get_role_oid()`, HBA rule matching\n3. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/auth.c:ClientAuthentication` (line 593) — Routes to `CheckPasswordAuth` based on HBA rule\n4. **Source**: `src/backend/libpq/auth.c:recv_password_packet` (line 732) — Untrusted password received from network\n5. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/crypt.c:get_role_password` (line 47) — Database lookup: `SearchSysCache1(AUTHNAME, PointerGetDatum(role))` retrieves stored password hash from pg_authid\n6. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/crypt.c:plain_crypt_verify` (lines 255-321) — Password verification\n   - For SCRAM-SHA-256: Calls `scram_verify_plain_password()` (line 271)\n   - For MD5: Hashes client password with `pg_md5_encrypt()` (line 286), compares with stored hash\n7. **Sink**: `src/backend/libpq/auth.c:set_authn_id` (line 814) — Session establishment: Records authenticated identity in `MyClientConnectionInfo.authn_id`\n\n### Flow 2: MD5 Challenge-Response Authentication\n1. **Source**: `src/backend/tcop/backend_startup.c:ProcessStartupPacket` — Untrusted user_name from startup packet\n2. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/hba.c:check_hba` — Role lookup, HBA rule matching\n3. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/auth.c:ClientAuthentication` (line 589) — Routes to `CheckPWChallengeAuth`\n4. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/auth.c:CheckPWChallengeAuth` (line 833) — Calls `get_role_password()` database lookup\n5. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/auth.c:CheckMD5Auth` (line 890) — Generates random salt via `pg_strong_random()`\n6. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/auth.c:sendAuthRequest` (line 897) — Sends salt to client\n7. **Source**: `src/backend/libpq/auth.c:recv_password_packet` (line 899) — Receives untrusted MD5-hashed response from network\n8. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/crypt.c:md5_crypt_verify` (lines 201-242) — Password verification\n   - Verifies password type is MD5 (line 213)\n   - Computes expected hash: `pg_md5_encrypt(shadow_pass + 3, md5_salt, 4)` (line 225)\n   - Compares client response with expected hash (line 233)\n9. **Sink**: `src/backend/libpq/auth.c:set_authn_id` — Session establishment\n\n### Flow 3: SCRAM-SHA-256 Challenge-Response Authentication\n1. **Source**: `src/backend/tcop/backend_startup.c:ProcessStartupPacket` — Untrusted user_name from startup packet\n2. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/hba.c:check_hba` — Role lookup, HBA rule matching\n3. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/auth.c:ClientAuthentication` (line 589) — Routes to `CheckPWChallengeAuth`\n4. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/auth.c:CheckPWChallengeAuth` (line 862) — Routes to `CheckSASLAuth(&pg_be_scram_mech)`\n5. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/auth-sasl.c:CheckSASLAuth` (line 68) — Sends `AUTH_REQ_SASL` with mechanism list\n6. **Source**: `src/backend/libpq/auth-sasl.c:CheckSASLAuth` (line 98) — Receives untrusted SASL tokens from network via `pq_getmessage()`\n7. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/auth-sasl.c:CheckSASLAuth` (line 117) — Parses selected mechanism from untrusted client data via `pq_getmsgrawstring()`\n8. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/auth-scram.c:scram_init` (line 131) — Initializes SCRAM mechanism\n   - Validates selected mechanism is SCRAM-SHA-256 or SCRAM-SHA-256-PLUS (lines 258-267)\n   - Database lookup: `get_password_type(shadow_pass)` (line 274)\n   - If user exists and has valid SCRAM secret: `parse_scram_secret()` (line 278) extracts iterations, salt, StoredKey, ServerKey from password hash\n   - **CRITICAL MITIGATION**: If user doesn't exist or password invalid: `mock_scram_secret()` (line 325) creates fake salt, iterations, StoredKey, ServerKey to prevent user enumeration\n9. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/auth-sasl.c:CheckSASLAuth` (line 157) — Calls `mech->exchange()` to process SASL tokens (loops until success)\n10. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/auth-scram.c:scram_exchange` — Processes SCRAM client messages, verifies client proof\n    - Parses client final message\n    - Verifies channel binding (if SCRAM-SHA-256-PLUS)\n    - Computes ClientKey = HMAC(SaltedPassword, \"Client Key\")\n    - Computes ClientSignature = HMAC(StoredKey, ClientAuthMessage)\n    - Extracts ClientProof from client message\n    - Verifies: ClientKey = ClientProof XOR ClientSignature (constant-time comparison)\n11. **Sink**: `src/backend/libpq/auth.c:set_authn_id` — Session establishment\n\n### Flow 4: User Enumeration Prevention (SCRAM and MD5)\n1. **Entry**: Client attempts authentication with non-existent user\n2. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/hba.c:check_hba` (line 2538) — `get_role_oid(port->user_name, true)` returns NULL for non-existent user (missing_ok=true)\n3. **Transform**: `src/backend/libpq/auth.c:CheckPWChallengeAuth` (line 833) — `get_role_password()` returns NULL for non-existent user\n4. **CRITICAL MITIGATION**:\n   - For SCRAM: `src/backend/libpq/auth-scram.c:scram_init` (lines 323-329) — Creates mock secret via `mock_scram_secret()` instead of failing early\n   - For MD5: `src/backend/libpq/auth.c:CheckPWChallengeAuth` (line 845) — Uses `Password_encryption` setting to choose mock password type\n5. **Transform**: Authentication proceeds with mock credentials\n6. **Sink**: Authentication fails identically to incorrect password case, no user enumeration leak\n\n## Dependency Chain\n\n```\nPostmaster (raw TCP acceptance)\n    ↓\nProcessStartupPacket (startup packet received)\n    ├→ Extracts user_name, database_name, application_name [ENTRY POINT 1]\n    ├→ Validates packet structure\n    └→ Stores in Port structure\n        ↓\n    ClientAuthentication (main auth dispatcher)\n        ├→ hba_getauthmethod\n        │   ├→ check_hba [ENTRY POINT 2]\n        │   │   ├→ get_role_oid(user_name) [database lookup]\n        │   │   ├→ Matches HBA rules\n        │   │   └→ Selects auth_method\n        │   └→ Returns auth_method\n        │\n        ├→ Switch on auth_method:\n        │\n        ├→ uaPassword:\n        │   └→ CheckPasswordAuth [ENTRY POINT 3]\n        │       ├→ sendAuthRequest(AUTH_REQ_PASSWORD)\n        │       ├→ recv_password_packet [ENTRY POINT 4]\n        │       ├→ get_role_password(user_name) [database lookup]\n        │       ├→ plain_crypt_verify\n        │       │   ├→ For SCRAM: scram_verify_plain_password\n        │       │   └→ For MD5: pg_md5_encrypt\n        │       └→ set_authn_id [session establishment]\n        │\n        ├→ uaMD5 / uaSCRAM:\n        │   └→ CheckPWChallengeAuth\n        │       ├→ get_role_password(user_name) [database lookup]\n        │       ├→ get_password_type(shadow_pass)\n        │       │\n        │       ├→ For MD5:\n        │       │   └→ CheckMD5Auth\n        │       │       ├→ pg_strong_random(salt)\n        │       │       ├→ sendAuthRequest(AUTH_REQ_MD5, salt)\n        │       │       ├→ recv_password_packet [ENTRY POINT 5]\n        │       │       ├→ md5_crypt_verify\n        │       │       │   └→ pg_md5_encrypt\n        │       │       └→ set_authn_id\n        │       │\n        │       └→ For SCRAM:\n        │           └→ CheckSASLAuth(&pg_be_scram_mech) [ENTRY POINT 6]\n        │               ├→ sendAuthRequest(AUTH_REQ_SASL, mechanism_list)\n        │               ├→ Loop SASL exchange:\n        │               │   ├→ recv SASLResponse [ENTRY POINT 7]\n        │               │   ├→ scram_init [ENTRY POINT 8]\n        │               │   │   ├→ Parse selected_mech from client [ENTRY POINT 9]\n        │               │   │   ├→ get_password_type(shadow_pass)\n        │               │   │   ├→ If valid: parse_scram_secret\n        │               │   │   │   └→ Extracts iterations, salt, StoredKey, ServerKey\n        │               │   │   └→ If invalid: mock_scram_secret [MITIGATION]\n        │               │   │       └→ Sets doomed=true\n        │               │   └→ scram_exchange\n        │               │       ├→ Parse client message\n        │               │       ├→ Verify channel binding\n        │               │       ├→ Compute ClientSignature = HMAC(StoredKey, ClientAuthMessage)\n        │               │       ├→ Verify ClientProof (constant-time comparison)\n        │               │       └→ Generate server proof\n        │               └→ set_authn_id [session establishment]\n        │\n        └→ Other methods (GSS, SSPI, Peer, Ident, LDAP, RADIUS, PAM, BSD, OAuth)\n```\n\n## Analysis\n\n### 1. Authentication Architecture\n\nPostgreSQL implements a modular authentication system where the authentication method is determined by the pg_hba.conf configuration file matching against the client's IP address, port, database, and user. The authentication pipeline has multiple entry points where untrusted client data enters:\n\n1. **Startup packet parsing**: User name, database name, application name\n2. **Password reception**: Plaintext password for `password` method\n3. **Challenge response**: MD5-hashed password for `md5` method\n4. **SASL/SCRAM tokens**: Binary SASL protocol messages for `scram-sha-256` method\n\nEach entry point has different validation and processing:\n- **Startup packet**: Validated for length and structure, names truncated to NAMEDATALEN\n- **Plaintext password**: No structural validation (arbitrary string)\n- **MD5 response**: Expected to be MD5 hash (32 hex chars)\n- **SCRAM tokens**: Binary SASL protocol with format validation in SCRAM implementation\n\n### 2. Role Validation\n\nThe role lookup happens via `get_role_oid(port->user_name, true)` in `check_hba()` with `missing_ok=true`. This is critical because:\n\n1. It allows the user_name to be validated against the pg_authid catalog\n2. However, the `missing_ok=true` flag means **non-existent users do not cause immediate failure**\n3. This is intentional to support user enumeration prevention through dummy authentication\n\nThe security model relies on never revealing whether a user exists or whether a password is correct for a non-existent user.\n\n### 3. Password Verification Mechanisms\n\n#### Plaintext Authentication (uaPassword)\n- **Flow**: Client sends password in plaintext → recv_password_packet → plain_crypt_verify\n- **Verification**:\n  - For SCRAM-SHA-256 passwords: Client password is hashed using stored salt and iterations, compared with StoredKey\n  - For MD5 passwords: Client password is hashed with user name, compared with stored hash\n- **Risk**: Password sent in plaintext over network (only safe if SSL/TLS encrypted)\n\n#### MD5 Challenge-Response (uaMD5)\n- **Flow**: Server sends random salt → Client responds with MD5(MD5(password + username) + salt) → md5_crypt_verify\n- **Verification**: Server computes same hash and compares\n- **Advantage**: Password not sent in plaintext (but salt is public)\n- **Disadvantage**: MD5 is cryptographically weak; vulnerability in 1-iteration MD5\n\n#### SCRAM-SHA-256 Challenge-Response (uaSCRAM)\n- **Flow**: Multi-message SASL exchange with HMAC-SHA-256 based proof\n- **Verification**:\n  - Server sends iterations, salt, ServerSignature\n  - Client computes SaltedPassword = PBKDF2-SHA-256(password, salt, iterations)\n  - Client sends ClientProof = ClientKey XOR ClientSignature\n  - Server verifies ClientProof using stored ClientKey\n- **Advantages**:\n  - Password not exposed (uses key derivation)\n  - Salted and iterated hashing (PBKDF2)\n  - Two-way authentication (server provides ServerSignature)\n  - Channel binding support (SCRAM-SHA-256-PLUS)\n\n### 4. User Enumeration Prevention\n\nPostgreSQL implements \"dummy authentication\" to prevent user enumeration attacks:\n\n**SCRAM Implementation** (auth-scram.c:scram_init lines 323-329):\n```c\nif (!got_secret) {\n    mock_scram_secret(state->port->user_name, &state->hash_type,\n                      &state->iterations, &state->key_length,\n                      &state->salt,\n                      state->StoredKey, state->ServerKey);\n    state->doomed = true;\n}\n```\n\n**MD5/SCRAM Selection** (auth.c:CheckPWChallengeAuth lines 844-845):\n```c\nif (!shadow_pass)\n    pwtype = Password_encryption;  // Use configured default\nelse\n    pwtype = get_password_type(shadow_pass);\n```\n\n**Effect**:\n- Authentication always proceeds with valid-looking (but incorrect) credentials\n- Authentication fails identically for non-existent users and wrong passwords\n- No timing differences reveal user existence\n- An attacker cannot enumerate valid usernames\n\n### 5. HBA Rule Matching\n\nThe HBA matching pipeline (hba.c:check_hba):\n\n1. **Connection type matching**: Check if connection is local (Unix socket), host, hostssl, or hostnossl\n2. **IP address matching**: If remote, verify client IP matches HBA rule's address/netmask using check_ip()\n3. **Hostname matching**: Optional reverse DNS lookup and hostname matching\n4. **Database matching**: Check if client's requested database matches HBA rule (or wildcard)\n5. **Role matching**: Check if authenticated role matches HBA rule (or wildcard)\n6. **First match wins**: Rules are processed in order; first match determines auth method\n\n**Security properties**:\n- Rules are configuration-based and static (loaded at startup or via SIGHUP reload)\n- Rules are evaluated in file order, allowing defaults and overrides\n- Implicit reject if no rule matches (uaImplicitReject)\n- Explicit reject allows security rules to be clear\n\n### 6. Data Validation at Entry Points\n\n#### Startup Packet (ProcessStartupPacket)\n- **Validation**: Length check (536 <= len <= MAX_STARTUP_PACKET_LENGTH), protocol version validation\n- **Truncation**: User name and database name truncated to NAMEDATALEN (63 bytes)\n- **Application name**: Cleaned via `pg_clean_ascii()` to prevent log injection\n- **GUC options**: Stored for later processing but not yet validated\n\n#### Password Packet (recv_password_packet)\n- **Validation**: Message type check, packet length validation, empty password rejection\n- **No encoding validation**: Passwords are treated as raw bytes (encoding determined later)\n- **Length limit**: PG_MAX_AUTH_TOKEN_LENGTH (typically 1GB or large value)\n\n#### SASL Tokens (CheckSASLAuth)\n- **Length limit**: mech->max_message_length (SCRAM uses 262144 bytes)\n- **Format validation**: Delegated to SASL mechanism implementation\n\n### 7. Vulnerability Classes and Mitigations\n\n#### User Enumeration (Timing-Based)\n- **Attack**: Measure response time differences to infer user existence\n- **Mitigation**: Dummy authentication - invalid users always proceed through full authentication exchange with mock credentials\n- **Residual risk**: SCRAM iteration count and salt generation timing could leak user existence with extreme precision\n- **Impact**: Network timing is dominated by random variation; differential timing < 1ms is hard to exploit\n\n#### User Enumeration (Information Leakage)\n- **Attack**: Inspect error messages to determine if user exists\n- **Mitigation**: Error messages don't differentiate between \"user doesn't exist\" and \"wrong password\"\n- **Implementation**: Errors logged to server log (not sent to client) via logdetail parameter\n\n#### Password Exposure in Transit\n- **Attack**: Plaintext password captured on network\n- **Mitigation**: SSL/TLS encryption required (uaPassword method highly discouraged without SSL)\n- **Better alternatives**: SCRAM-SHA-256 (challenge-response, password never sent)\n\n#### Dictionary Attacks / Brute Force\n- **Attack**: Attacker tries many passwords\n- **Mitigation**: SCRAM-SHA-256 uses PBKDF2 with iteration count (server-side rate limiting not implemented)\n- **Residual risk**: PostgreSQL doesn't implement connection rate limiting or attempt counting at authentication stage\n- **Recommended**: Configure firewall/proxy to rate-limit authentication attempts\n\n#### MITM Attack (Password Exposure)\n- **Attack**: Attacker intercepts MD5 challenge-response exchange\n- **Risk**: MD5 is weak; reversing password from hash may be feasible for weak passwords\n- **Mitigation**: SCRAM-SHA-256 uses salted PBKDF2 (iteration count × password strength required)\n- **Better mitigation**: Enforce SSL/TLS for all connections\n\n#### Channel Binding (TLS Hijacking)\n- **Attack**: Attacker hijacks TLS session after authentication\n- **Mitigation**: SCRAM-SHA-256-PLUS includes channel binding (tls-server-end-point)\n- **Implementation**: Binds SCRAM authentication to TLS certificate (prevents MitM)\n- **Requirement**: Must use SCRAM-SHA-256-PLUS variant (not default)\n\n#### Role Privilege Escalation\n- **Attack**: Authenticated user tries to access another role's data/privileges\n- **Mitigation**: Session role is set to authenticated user (set_authn_id); authorization checks use this role\n- **Scope**: Authentication only verifies user identity; authorization is separate (GRANT/REVOKE)\n\n### 8. Existing Mitigations Summary\n\n| Vulnerability | Mitigation | Strength |\n|---|---|---|\n| User enumeration | Dummy authentication (SCRAM, MD5) | Strong |\n| Password in transit | SSL/TLS encryption + SCRAM | Strong if TLS enforced |\n| Weak password hashing | PBKDF2 + salt + iterations (SCRAM) | Strong |\n| MD5 weakness | Deprecated but still supported | Weak (legacy) |\n| Plaintext password method | Deprecated but configurable | Weak (legacy) |\n| MITM / channel hijacking | SCRAM-SHA-256-PLUS + channel binding | Strong if PLUS variant used |\n| Timing attacks | Constant-time HMAC comparison | Strong (implementation detail) |\n| Log injection via password | Passwords not logged | Strong |\n| Log injection via application_name | Sanitized with pg_clean_ascii() | Strong |\n| Invalid SCRAM secret | Graceful fallback to dummy | Strong |\n| User doesn't exist | Dummy authentication (no error) | Strong |\n\n### 9. Attack Scenarios\n\n#### Scenario 1: User Enumeration Attack\n**Attacker goal**: Determine valid usernames\n\n**Attack attempt**:\n1. Connect to PostgreSQL with user \"admin\"\n2. Send random password\n3. Check if error message differs from connecting with \"nonexistent_user\"\n\n**Defense**:\n- Error messages don't differentiate: both receive \"authentication failed\"\n- Authentication always proceeds with valid-looking (but incorrect) credentials\n- Timing is identical (within network variation)\n- **Result**: Attack fails; attacker cannot enumerate users\n\n#### Scenario 2: Brute Force Password Attack\n**Attacker goal**: Guess password for known user\n\n**Attack attempt**:\n1. Connect with user \"postgres\"\n2. Try 10,000 passwords rapidly\n\n**Defense**:\n- PostgreSQL doesn't implement built-in rate limiting\n- Each password attempt requires full SCRAM exchange (computational cost on server)\n- SCRAM uses PBKDF2 with ~4096 iterations (default)\n- Network latency dominates\n- **Recommended mitigation**: Firewall/proxy implements rate limiting, fail2ban, or PgBouncer\n\n#### Scenario 3: MITM Password Hijacking\n**Attacker goal**: Capture password for offline cracking\n\n**Attack attempt**:\n1. Position MITM between client and server\n2. Intercept authentication exchange\n\n**Defense**:\n- SCRAM-SHA-256: Password not transmitted; hash of password transmitted\n- SCRAM-SHA-256-PLUS: Binds to TLS certificate; MITM causes different channel binding value\n- **If SSL/TLS not enforced**: Plaintext MD5 challenge-response vulnerable\n- **Recommended mitigation**: Require SSL/TLS for all connections (force_ssl=on)\n\n#### Scenario 4: Timing-Based User Enumeration\n**Attacker goal**: Detect user existence via response timing\n\n**Attack attempt**:\n1. Measure response time for \"user doesn't exist\" case\n2. Measure response time for \"wrong password\" case\n3. Correlate timing differences\n\n**Defense**:\n- Dummy authentication ensures both paths take similar time\n- SCRAM iteration count is fixed per password type\n- Salt generation (random) occurs regardless\n- **Residual risk**: Iteration count could theoretically leak user existence with statistical analysis over many attempts\n- **Mitigation effectiveness**: Network jitter (~10ms) overwhelms auth timing differences (~0.1ms)\n\n#### Scenario 5: HBA Rule Bypass\n**Attacker goal**: Bypass HBA rules by spoofing IP address\n\n**Attack attempt**:\n1. Attempt connection from different IP address\n2. Claim to be from trusted network\n\n**Defense**:\n- HBA matching occurs after TCP connection is established\n- Client's real IP (from socket) is used; client cannot claim different IP\n- Source IP verification is provided by OS-level networking\n- **Additional defense**: Firewall can enforce network segmentation\n\n#### Scenario 6: SASL Mechanism Downgrade\n**Attacker goal**: Force downgrade to weaker SCRAM-SHA-256 variant without channel binding\n\n**Attack attempt**:\n1. Advertise both SCRAM-SHA-256 and SCRAM-SHA-256-PLUS\n2. Client selects SCRAM-SHA-256 (no channel binding)\n3. Continue with weaker authentication\n\n**Defense**:\n- Server advertises only mechanisms supported for the connection\n- If SSL/TLS in use: Server advertises SCRAM-SHA-256-PLUS\n- If no SSL/TLS: Server advertises SCRAM-SHA-256 (no channel binding possible)\n- Client cannot select mechanism that server didn't advertise\n- **Mitigations**:\n  - Use SSL/TLS for all connections\n  - Require SCRAM-SHA-256-PLUS if possible\n\n### 10. Gaps and Recommendations\n\n#### Gap 1: No Built-in Rate Limiting\n- **Current**: PostgreSQL has no per-user or per-IP authentication attempt limit\n- **Recommendation**:\n  - Use PgBouncer connection pooling with built-in rate limiting\n  - Deploy fail2ban or similar on host\n  - Implement firewall rules (ip_based_gatekeeper, iptables, cloud WAF)\n\n#### Gap 2: Legacy Authentication Methods\n- **Current**: `password` (plaintext) and `md5` methods still supported\n- **Risk**: Weak protection if SSL/TLS not enforced\n- **Recommendation**:\n  - Upgrade to SCRAM-SHA-256 for all roles\n  - Disable uaPassword and uaMD5 methods in pg_hba.conf\n  - Set password_encryption = scram-sha-256 globally\n\n#### Gap 3: SCRAM-SHA-256-PLUS Adoption\n- **Current**: PLUS variant (channel binding) available but not required\n- **Risk**: Vulnerable to MITM if only SCRAM-SHA-256 is used\n- **Recommendation**:\n  - Require SSL/TLS for all connections (ssl=on in postgresql.conf)\n  - Prefer SCRAM-SHA-256-PLUS when available\n  - Monitor server logs for PLUS variant usage\n\n#### Gap 4: User Enumeration via Indirect Channels\n- **Current**: Direct authentication reveals no user info, but other channels might\n- **Risk**: Database schema queries, role listing, replication slot names can reveal roles\n- **Recommendation**:\n  - Restrict access to system catalogs for unauthenticated connections\n  - Use views to hide sensitive role information\n  - Monitor unusual query patterns for probing\n\n#### Gap 5: No Cryptographic Hardware Acceleration for PBKDF2\n- **Current**: PBKDF2 computation is CPU-bound\n- **Risk**: Slow brute-force attacks remain slow (not an exploit, but defense could be stronger)\n- **Recommendation**:\n  - Increase iteration count for SCRAM-SHA-256 (if acceptable for performance)\n  - Use HSM or dedicated crypto accelerator if available\n  - Monitor SCRAM authentication latency\n\n#### Gap 6: Application Name Injection Prevention Only\n- **Current**: Only application_name is sanitized; user_name and database_name are not\n- **Risk**: Multiline log entries could be injected if parsed unsafely\n- **Recommendation**:\n  - Server logs application_name (sanitized) but logs are trusted source\n  - No identified vulnerability; mitigation is already in place\n\n## Summary\n\nPostgreSQL's client authentication pipeline is well-designed with multiple security layers:\n\n1. **Entry points** are clearly defined and validated:\n   - Startup packet: Length and structure validation\n   - Password/SASL tokens: Delegated to authentication method\n   - HBA matching: Configuration-based, not client input\n\n2. **Data flow** is clear and controlled:\n   - Untrusted input → Database lookup → Verification → Session establishment\n   - Password never stored in plaintext\n   - Passwords compared via cryptographic hashes\n\n3. **User enumeration** is prevented via dummy authentication:\n   - Non-existent users proceed through full authentication exchange\n   - Identical failure modes for missing users and wrong passwords\n   - Timing differences minimized\n\n4. **Password verification** uses strong cryptography:\n   - SCRAM-SHA-256: PBKDF2-SHA-256 with salt and iterations\n   - MD5: Weak but salted challenge-response (deprecated)\n   - Plaintext: Only safe over SSL/TLS\n\n5. **Security architecture** is modular:\n   - Multiple authentication methods selectable per HBA rule\n   - Methods are isolated and cannot interfere\n   - Fallback to implicit reject if no rule matches\n\n**Key vulnerabilities to monitor**:\n- Insufficient rate limiting (external mitigation required)\n- Legacy authentication methods (must be disabled manually)\n- MITM exposure without SSL/TLS (must be enforced)\n- User enumeration via indirect channels (schema introspection)\n\n**Recommended deployment**:\n1. Enforce SSL/TLS for all connections\n2. Migrate all users to SCRAM-SHA-256 passwords\n3. Disable `password` and `md5` authentication methods\n4. Implement firewall-level rate limiting\n5. Monitor authentication logs for anomalous patterns\n6. Use PgBouncer or similar for connection pooling and additional security controls\n"},"caller":{"type":"direct"}}],"stop_reason":null,"stop_sequence":null,"usage":{"input_tokens":8,"cache_creation_input_tokens":659,"cache_read_input_tokens":79458,"cache_creation":{"ephemeral_5m_input_tokens":0,"ephemeral_1h_input_tokens":659},"output_tokens":1,"service_tier":"standard","inference_geo":"not_available"},"context_management":null},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"81c70249-7bd7-4c59-a795-02ba0487c322","uuid":"899f0924-a458-414e-8ff8-a5ddc156c1d4"}

PostgreSQL Client Authentication Pipeline Security Analysis\n\n## Files Examined\n\n- `src/backend/libpq/auth.c` — Main authentication entry point and dispatcher for all authentication methods\n- `src/backend/libpq/auth-scram.c` — SCRAM-SHA-256 SASL mechanism implementation\n- `src/backend/libpq/auth-sasl.c` — SASL framework for challenge-response authentication\n- `src/backend/libpq/crypt.c` — Password cryptography and verification (plaintext, MD5, SCRAM)\n- `src/backend/libpq/hba.c` — Host-Based Authentication (HBA) configuration parsing and rule matching\n- `src/backend/libpq/pqcomm.c` — Protocol communication and message I/O\n- `src/backend/tcop/backend_startup.c` — Startup packet processing and initial connection setup\n- `src/backend/libpq/be-secure.c` — SSL/TLS security layer\n- `src/backend/postmaster/postmaster.c` — Postmaster main loop and connection acceptance\n- `src/include/libpq/auth.h` — Authentication subsystem public header\n\n## Entry Points\n\n1. `port->application_name`\n   - Validation: Checks packet length (535-542), validates protocol version (705-713), validates user name is provided (827-830), truncates names to NAMEDATALEN\n\n3.
